This instruction implements Air Force Policy Directive (AFPD) 23-1, Materiel Management
Policy and Procedures. Establishes Headquarters United States Air Force (HQ USAF) Vehicle
Management procedures and describes the supporting data systems. Vehicle Fleet Manager
(VFM) or Vehicle Management Superintendent (VMS) expand or revise these procedures to fit
local circumstances, within the intent of this instruction. Objectives, policies and responsibilities
of Vehicle Management derive from overarching policy in Department of Defense Directive
(DODD) 4500.36, Management, Acquisition, and Use of Motor Vehicles, August 3, 2004 and
Department of Defense (DOD) 4140.50-R, Management and Standards of DOD locomotives, 19
June 1985, DOD 4500.36-R, Management, Acquisition, and Use of Motor Vehicles, March 16,
2007, and DOD 4500.51, Commercially Procured and Leased Armored Vehicle Policy, July 25,
2007.     Send comments for suggested improvements on Air Force (AF) Form 847,
Recommendation for Change of Publication, to HQ USAF/A4LE, 1030 Air Force Pentagon,
Washington, DC 20330-1030. Ensure that all records created as a result of processes prescribed
in this publication are maintained in accordance with AFMAN 37-123 (will convert to AFMAN
33-363), Management of Records, and disposed of in accordance with the Air Force Records
2                                                            AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


Disposition Schedule (RDS) located at https://afrims.amc.af.mil/. This publication is applicable
to the Air National Guard (ANG) as well as the Air Force Reserve (AFR); however, it does not
apply to the Civil Air Patrol (CAP). The use of the name or mark of any specific manufacture,
commercial product, commodity or service in this publication does not imply endorsement by the
Air Force.

(PACAF) AFI 23-302 is supplemented as follows: This supplement applies to all United
States Air Force (USAF) vehicle management functions within Pacific Air Forces. Deviations
from the procedures described herein are prohibited without written approval from the Office of
Primary Responsibility (OPR). All reporting requirements listed in this supplement are exempt
from licensing IAW, AFI 33-324, para2.11.6, The Information Collections and Reports
Management Program; Controlling Internal, Public, and Interagency Air Force Information
Collections. Refer recommended changes and questions about this publication to the OPR using
Air Force Form 847, Recommendation for Change of Publication; route AF Forms 847 from the
field through the appropriate functional chain of command. Ensure that all records created as a
result of processes prescribed in this publication are maintained in accordance with Air Force
Manual (AFMAN) 33-363, Management of Records, and disposed of in accordance with Air
Force Records Information Management System (AFRIMS) Records Disposition Schedule
(RDS) located at https://www.my.af.mil/gcss-af61a/afrims/afrims/. This supplement does not
apply to the Air National Guard (ANG) and the Air Force Reserve Command (AFRC).

SUMMARY OF CHANGES

This interim change (IC) incorporates AFI 23-302_AFGM1, Guidance Memorandum on
Registered Equipment Management System (REMS) Transformation to a Centralized Registered
Equipment Management System (C-REMS). Additionally, this IC updates office symbols and
implements new, and supplements current, AF Vehicle Management guidance, policies and/or
programs for the following areas: alternative fuel utilization, Vehicle Control Program, DOD
Fleet Credit Card System, Allowance Standards (AS) 010, AF Form 601, Equipment Action
Request, approval authority, Vehicle Assessment Inspection, Standardized Vehicle Management
Functional and Nuclear Certified Vehicle Checklists. Areas removed from this instruction
include: AS 019, the Registered Equipment Management function and general Vehicle Official
Use rules.

(PACAF) This supplement realigns the numbering system due to a complete realignment of AFI
23-302. Multiple items from the previous supplement were incorporated into AFI 23-302
allowing for deletion. This publication has been substantially rewritten and requires complete
review before implementation.

                                            Chapter 1

 INTRODUCTION GENERAL INFORMATION AND ADMINISTRATION, TYPES OF
           MAINTENANCE AND ITEMS OF SPECIAL INTEREST

Section 1A—General Information and Administration

1.1. Principles of Vehicle Management. Use economical, reliable and up-to-date procedures
to authorize, acquire, account for and maintain vehicles in a safe and serviceable condition
throughout their lifecycle.
     1.1.1. Motor Vehicle Management Definitions.
        1.1.1.1. Vehicle. ―Motor vehicle‖ means any vehicle, self propelled or drawn by
        mechanical power, designed and operated principally for highway transportation of
        property or passengers, but does not include a military design motor vehicle or vehicles.
        1.1.1.2. Military Design (Tactical) Vehicle. ―Military design motor vehicle‖ means a
        motor vehicle designed according to military or commercial specifications to directly
        support combat or tactical operations or training for such operations.
        1.1.1.3. Reportable Vehicle. ―Registered Equipment Management System‖ (REMS)
        reportable vehicles include passenger carrying, cargo and utility, special purpose, low
        speed vehicle (LSV), fire fighting, aircraft refueling, materiel handling equipment and
        base maintenance vehicles/ equipment of commercial or military design. Vehicular type
        equipment that meets any of the following general descriptions is included in this
        definition.
            1.1.1.3.1. Designed or used for military field training, combat or tactical purposes.
            1.1.1.3.2. Used principally within the confines of a regularly established Air Force
            Base, target range or depot.
            1.1.1.3.3. Used by an agency in the performance of investigative, law enforcement or
            intelligence duties if the head of such agency determines that exclusive control of
            such vehicle is essential to the effective performance of such duties.
     1.1.2. Environment Protection Act of 1992/2005 (EPAct), Executive Order (EO) 13423, and
     Public Laws 107-107 and 105-388 reportable categories and vehicle definitions. NOTE: The
     General Services Administration (GSA), the Department of Energy (DOE) and the Office of
     Management and Budget (OMB) have the right to change the types of vehicles that are
     reported each year based on Congressional and Executive office of the President
     requirements.
        1.1.2.1. Vehicle categories required for Energy Policy Act (EPAct) and Executive Order
        (EO) reporting purposes (i.e. Annual Federal Automotive Statistical Tool (FAST), Office
        of Management and Budget (OMB) A11-33 reports and SF82, Annual Agency Report of
        Motor Vehicle Data). The Air Force has Commercial/GSA leased and Agency Owned
        Vehicles.
AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009                                                         17


           1.1.2.1.1. Light Duty vehicles and trucks. Commercially designed licensable over the
           road vehicles with a Gross Vehicle Weight Rating (GVWR) of less than 8,501
           pounds.
           1.1.2.1.2. Medium Duty vehicles and trucks. Commercially designed licensable over
           the road vehicles with a GVWR of more than 8,500 pounds and less than 16,001
           pounds.
           1.1.2.1.3. Heavy Duty vehicles and trucks. Commercially designed licensable over
           the road vehicles with a GVWR over 16,000 pounds. Example, truck tractors, over
           the road dump trucks, structural fire trucks commercially designed for road travel
           would be reportable, however airport crash recovering firefighting vehicles would
           not.
       1.1.2.2. Vehicles reported in FAST but exempt from EPAct and EO mandates. Data is
       used only for the OMB A11-33.
           1.1.2.2.1. Law Enforcement vehicles: Law Enforcement vehicles are those that have
           been equipped or retrofitted with emergency lights/siren along with other
           modifications such as heavy-duty or high performance suspensions and drive trains.
           The EPAct/EO exemption also covers vehicles that may be used occasionally for the
           purpose of surveillance or undercover operations (i.e. Air Force Office of Special
           Investigations (AFOSI) vehicles) or that will be transferred into law enforcement
           service in the future.
           1.1.2.2.2. Emergency vehicles such as ambulances and structural fire trucks.
           1.1.2.2.3. Non-Tactical Armored Vehicles: OMB requires each agency to report
           their commercially designed armored vehicles and the armor classification. Armor
           levels classifications are as defined in National Institute of Justice (NIJ) Standard
           0108.01.
       1.1.2.3. Vehicles that are not to be reported in FAST: Vehicles that do not fall under the
       data requirements of the Agency Report of Motor Vehicles to the OMB (OMB SF82
       Report), the EPAct of 1992/2005 and Executive Order 13423 include the following:
           1.1.2.3.1. Military tactical vehicles: Motor vehicles acquired and used for military
           purposes that the Secretary of Defense has certified must be exempt for national
           security reasons; (war fighting vehicles - shooters, troop transportation, tactical
           equipment and lorries and engineering vehicles).
           1.1.2.3.2. Any petroleum fueled off road vehicle (e.g., construction/engineering
           vehicles, tractors, mowers, Material Handling Equipment (forklifts), aircraft
           servicing/refueling, Snow Removal vehicles (plows), LSV, etc). These vehicles are
           not licensable for use on federal highway.
           1.1.2.3.3. Any other class or type of vehicles the OMB determines to be exempt from
           reporting requirements.
1.2. Supplements. Major Command (MAJCOM) Vehicle Management Staffs may supplement
this instruction to provide detailed instructions to meet their needs, if they have HQ USAF/A4LE
approval prior to publication. MAJCOMs must route all supplements through the Vehicle and
18                                           AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


Equipment Management Support Office (VEMSO) for review and forwarding to HQ
USAF/A4LE.
     1.2.1. (Added-PACAF) . Subordinate unit and base level supplements to this volume will
     not be published without prior written approval from PACAF/A4RV.
1.3. Changes to this Instruction. Send recommendations to improve this instruction through
the MAJCOM Vehicle Management Staff to VEMSO for review and forwarding to HQ USAF/
A4LE for consideration.
1.4. Operating Instructions (OIs). Bases set and implement local guidance in the form of OIs
in accordance with (IAW) AFI 33-360, Publications and Forms Management.
     1.4.1. Coordinate these instructions with appropriate agencies and review or update as
     required by the VFM or VMS.
     1.4.2. Typically, OIs are established for hours of operation, housekeeping, safety, security,
     fire prevention, minimum essential repairs, quality control procedures, hazardous and solid
     waste management, and pollution prevention procedures.
     1.4.3. All assigned personnel acknowledge in writing (i.e., initials or signature, and date)
     signifying they understand the OI within 30 days of arrival, annually or when any instruction
     is changed.
     1.4.4. At a minimum, VFM or VMS must establish OIs for the following areas:
        1.4.4.1. Minimum essential repairs (refer to paragraph 1.19).
        1.4.4.2. Quality assurance procedures (refer to paragraphs 3.9.3, 3.10.33, 3.19, 3.19.7.6,
        4.71.1 and 7.19).
        1.4.4.3. Pollution prevention, in accordance with the installation Pollution Prevention
        Plan, and hazardous waste management, in accordance with AFI 32-7042, Solid and
        Hazardous Waste Compliance, and the installation Hazardous Waste Management Plan
        (refer to paragraph 1.16.3.4).
        1.4.4.4. Tire and battery procurement, issue,           disposal   and    tire   recapping
        policies/procedures (refer to paragraph 5.42).
        1.4.4.5. Tool control and accountability procedures (refer to paragraph 5.41.1.1).
        1.4.4.6. ―Maintenance Purposes Only‖ vehicle operator certification (refer to paragraph
        3.14). This is not applicable to contract operated vehicle management activities.
        1.4.4.7. Process for control of vehicle license plates (refer to paragraphs 4.20.7, 4.40
        and TO 36-1-191).
        1.4.4.8. (DELETED) .
        1.4.4.9. Fleet/Wright Express (WEX) Fuel Card program administration (if applicable)
        (refer to paragraph 4.4.4).
        1.4.4.10. (Added-PACAF) . An Operational Instruction (OI) is required for seasonal
        rebuild programs to include start and completion dates. These dates should be consistent
        with the using organization’s requirements. Note: This OI must be reviewed and signed
        by the Logistics Readiness Squadron Commander or equivalent.
AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009                                                           19


       1.4.4.11. (Added-PACAF) . Establish an OI detailing where and how road tests are
       performed. All vehicles being road tested by vehicle management will be properly
       identified by a placard/sign indicating ―ROAD TEST‖ or ―Operational Check‖. Fully
       coordinate road test route with installation ground safety and security forces units.
       1.4.4.12. (Added-PACAF) . In flights where foreign nationals are employed, OIs will
       be bilingual (English and host country language) unless the ability to read and
       comprehend English is a condition of employment. OIs not applicable to foreign national
       employees may be exempted from this requirement. If new OIs are introduced or when
       OIs are changed, foreign national employees may be verbally briefed on changes and
       briefings documented until written bilingual versions of these OIs are translated and
       available.
1.5. Use of Related Publications. See Attachment 1 for a list of referenced publications.
Management controls, records administration and forms documentation are described in many of
these publications.

Section 1B— Types of Maintenance

1.6. Base-Level Maintenance. Base-level maintenance includes both organizational and
intermediate levels of maintenance. Vehicle Management does not maintain everything on the
base with wheels and an engine. Upkeep of certain miscellaneous items of ground support
equipment or vehicle attachments is the responsibility of the unit that owns or uses it. Note: If
the attachment was originally purchased with the vehicle then Vehicle Management has
maintenance responsibility.
1.7. Organizational Maintenance. Organizational maintenance is accomplished by unit-level
vehicle operators in the various squadrons and activities across an installation. Operators use the
vehicle’s applicable Operator’s Inspection Guide and Trouble Report and applicable Technical
Orders (TOs) when accomplishing organizational maintenance. Specific organizational
responsibilities are outlined in paragraph 3.13
1.8. Intermediate Maintenance. Intermediate maintenance is accomplished by the base
Vehicle Management, including major and minor repairs. VFM or VMS determine shop
capability and decide which source of repair to use. Manpower, total cost, mission requirements,
Non Mission Capable (NMC) time, shop tools, equipment and technical capability influence the
decision making. Normally done in fixed shops, intermediate maintenance includes:
   1.8.1. Preventive Maintenance and Inspections (PM&Is) and Special Inspections.
       1.8.1.1. (Added-PACAF) . Crane load tests will be conducted In Accordance With
       (IAW) vehicle manufacturer T.O. In the absence of sufficient technical information from
       the manufacturer, refer to T.O. 36-1-191, Technical and Managerial Reference for Motor
       Vehicle Maintenance, paragraph 3.10.5.1 and AFOSH Standard 91-46, Materials
       Handling and Storage Equipment, paragraph 8.2.6.2.
   1.8.2. Repairing, fabricating or replacing unserviceable components.
   1.8.3. Refinishing, modifying (as directed by TO), repairing accessories and auxiliary
   equipment, and doing structural repair.
   1.8.4. Furnishing technical assistance to using organizations.
20                                               AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


     1.8.5. When repair exceeds the base capability, maintenance may be performed:
        1.8.5.1. By a local off-base contract.
        1.8.5.2. At a nearby AF base. (Comply with AFI 25-201, Support Agreements, if
        personnel adjustments are needed.)
        1.8.5.3. Under Inter-Service Support Agreement (ISSA) at nearby military or other
        government shops. Affected commanders or equivalents concur on cost and vehicle
        disposition issues.
1.9. Depot-Level Maintenance. This level of maintenance provides technical aid (see TO 00-
25-4, Depot Maintenance of Aerospace Vehicles and Training Equipment) and overhaul of
specific vehicles (see TO 36-1-191, Technical and Managerial Reference for Motor Vehicle
Maintenance).      Warner-Robins Air Logistics Center (WR-ALC) manages depot-level
maintenance for AF needs.
1.10. Mobile Maintenance. The nature of certain vehicle repairs requires maintenance support
away from the Vehicle Management Flight. The vehicle repair technician should debrief the
supervisor in charge or the vehicle operator concerning vehicle deficiencies. Review the
Operator’s Inspection Guide and Trouble Report for annotated maintenance problems.
     1.10.1. Mobile Maintenance can be used to perform PM&Is and Special Inspections on
     vehicles not easily brought to or unable to enter the maintenance shop due to size. Advance
     coordination is essential for proper planning and scheduling. Accomplish a complete
     inspection of the vehicle or equipment item at the time of the PM&I and Special Inspections.
     1.10.2. The Mobile Maintenance truck can be equipped with a tow attachment, a battery
     booster and power plant, per Allowance Standard (AS) 457. The truck should carry tools (AS
     403 and 457) and spare parts so that full PM&I and routine unscheduled work can be done.
     This truck should be two-way radio and/or cell phone equipped.
     1.10.3. The VFM or VMS has the option to expand Mobile Maintenance to include
     performing minor maintenance at selected units. It may be desirable to send a mobile
     maintenance truck to large organizations (Civil Engineering Squadron (CES), Security
     Forces Squadron (SFS), Aircraft Maintenance, etc.) on a scheduled frequency to perform
     minor repairs.
     1.10.4. The VFM or VMS determines, and then justifies, the number of Mobile Maintenance
     trucks necessary to be assigned.
     1.10.5. If the required repairs are beyond Mobile Maintenance capabilities, request wrecker
     assistance from Vehicle Operations. During other than normal operating hours, Vehicle
     Operations will deliver the disabled vehicle to the Vehicle Management parking area. The
     Operators’ Inspection Guide and Trouble Report will reflect the vehicle’s problem and must
     accompany the vehicle. During the next duty day, someone from the using organization must
     formally turn the vehicle in to Vehicle Management. Note: NMC begins when disabled
     vehicle is reported to Vehicle Management so if repairs cannot be completed in 2 hours or
     less open a work order to capture all NMC hours.
1.11. Scheduled Maintenance. Scheduled maintenance includes PM&I and Special
Inspections, at regular intervals to maintain a safe and serviceable vehicle fleet. The 18-Month
PM&I will be conducted using the AF Form 4354, Preventative Maintenance and Inspections, in
AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009                                                         21


conjunction with applicable manufacturer maintenance requirements. Technicians must use AF
Form 4355, Vehicle Incoming Inspection, to verify a vehicle’s condition when accepting a
vehicle in to maintenance. The use of AF Form 4355 will satisfy the USAF’s obligation to
inspect a vehicle’s ability to operate safely, at least once within a 12-month period, IAW DOD
4500.36-R. TO 36-1-191 prescribes scheduled maintenance intervals and is used to establish a
scheduled maintenance plan. Using organizations will make vehicles available for PM&Is and
Special Inspections or arrange for a rescheduled time so they can be completed before their due
dates. Using organizations that exhibit a trend of failing to provide vehicles for scheduled
maintenance at the scheduled time should be reported to the next command level (i.e., Mission
Support Group Commander, Wing Commander, etc.) for corrective action. Do not delay PM&Is,
Special Inspections or any special scheduled maintenance actions.
   1.11.1. Consider seasonal needs, labor-hour availability, organizational need and fair
   apportionment of the 18-month requirement when developing a scheduled maintenance plan.
   All shops may use On-Line Vehicle Integrated Management System (OLVIMS) products or
   other automated listings to help complete work on time. Scheduled maintenance has priority
   and is not delayed. When necessary, resort to a two-shift operation, overtime or contract to
   fulfill this need.
       1.11.1.1. (Added-PACAF) . Excludes all vehicles assigned to the SFS. Vehicle
       Maintenance Manager (VMM) has authority to change additional Periodic Maintenance
       and Inspection (PM&I) intervals (must be more stringent) based on mission requirements
       (i.e., summer rebuild, seasonal equipment, extreme high use,). All local changes will be
       in writing stating justification and included in the applicable vehicle record jacket.
   1.11.2. Vehicles used as static training aids (not operated on-base or public highways or for
   driver training) are exempt from scheduled maintenance. Also, vehicles such as mobile
   communication vans positioned in a semi-permanent or permanent site are exempt. Ensure
   vehicle is safe to operate before operating over public roads.
       1.11.2.1. Ensure training aids are on equipment account list.
   1.11.3. (Added-PACAF) . War Reserve Material (WRM) assets that are overdue scheduled
   maintenance intervals will have an active work order opened on the first day of the month
   after they become overdue. The work order will remain open until all discrepancies affecting
   the safe and serviceable operation of the vehicle are corrected. Note: Vehicles placed in deep
   storage using Vapor-phased Corrosion Inhibitor (VpCI) technology will have scheduled
   maintenance intervals adjusted IAW procedures outlined in PACAF Handbook 24-3,
   Preservation and Storage of War Reserve Material Vehicles and Equipment.
   1.11.4. (Added-PACAF) . Crane Inspection.
       1.11.4.1. (Added-PACAF) . In addition to inspection criteria in T.O. 36-1-191 and
       vehicle manufacturer T.O., cranes will be inspected IAW AFOSH Standard 91-46,
       paragraph 8.2.4.4.
       1.11.4.2. (Added-PACAF) . Crane hooks will be inspected IAW vehicle manufacturer
       T.O. In the absence of guidance from the manufacturer, inspect IAW AFOSH Standard
       91-46, paragraph 9.4.1.1 and 9.4.1.2.
22                                           AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


        1.11.4.3. (Added-PACAF) . An annual nondestructive inspection (NDI) is required on
        all nuclear certified crane hooks IAW AFOSH Standard 91-46, paragraph 9.4.1.4. NDI
        lab results will be maintained in the vehicle historical record until a new test is
        conducted. The Vehicle Fleet Manager (VFM) or Vehicle Management Superintendent
        (VMS) can expand requirement to include non-nuclear certified crane hooks.
1.12. Transient Vehicle and Equipment Maintenance. Provide maintenance priority to
transient vehicles according to their mission or circumstance. Note: The procedures for vehicle
operators to have work done on vehicles in-transit are in Air Force Joint Manual (AFJMAN) 24-
306, Manual for the Wheeled Vehicle Driver.

Section 1C— Items of Special Interest

1.13. Alternative Fueled Vehicles (AFVs). The EPAct of 1992 (Public Law 102-486), Title
VII of EPAct of 2005, (Public Law 109-58) and Executive Order (EO) 13423 ―Strengthening
Federal Environmental. Energy and Transportation Management," signed 24 January 2007,
direct Federal agencies to acquire specific minimum levels of AFVs, mandates the use of
alternative fuels 100 percent of the time in AFVs when appropriate, requires increasing our
alternative fuels consumption by 10% a year through FY 2015 using FY2005 as the baseline,
requires reducing total consumption of petroleum products by 2% a year through 2015 using
FY2005 as the baseline and requires the use of plug in hybrids (PIH) when vehicles PIH vehicles
are commercially available at a cost reasonably comparable, on the basis of life-cycle cost, to
non PIH vehicles. These mandates were established to improve the Nation’s air quality and
reduce our dependence on foreign oil. Our national strategy is to create a Federal fleet which
drives the country to commercialize AFVs and the infrastructure to support them. Department of
Defense Instruction (DODI) 4715.4, Pollution Prevention, 7 Jun 98, further requires that the
Secretaries of the Military Departments establish programs to purchase and operate AFVs IAW
EPAct.
     1.13.1. Acquisition. EPAct specifically applies to general purpose Light Duty Vehicles
     (LDVs) (less than or equal to 8,500 pounds Gross Vehicle Weight (GVW)) based in
     Metropolitan Statistical Areas (MSAs) (cities, counties, or consolidated areas with a 1980
     population of more than 250,000). AFV requirements for each MSA installation in a given
     Fiscal Year (FY) are calculated by multiplying the number of LDVs procured (new
     acquisitions and long-term leases) times the EPAct percentage required for that FY. 75
     percent of all MSA-based LDVs acquired each year must be AFVs. Note: The Energy
     Conservation and Reauthorization Act of 1998 amended EPAct of 1992 to allow one AFV
     acquisition credit for every 450 gallons of pure biodiesel (B100) and one credit for every
     2,250 gallons of blended biodiesel that consists of 20 percent biodiesel (B20) fuel consumed
     in vehicles over 8,500 pounds GVW rating. ―Biodiesel credits‖ may fulfill up to 50 percent
     of our agency’s vehicle acquisition EPAct requirements.
        1.13.1.1. Exemptions. Section 303(b) (3) of EPAct of 1992 exempts most law
        enforcement vehicles, emergency vehicles and vehicles used for military purposes
        (exempt for national security reasons) from AFV acquisition requirements. VEMSO will
        serve as the clearinghouse for exemption requests, exemption data collection and
        exemption reporting. The following instructions are based on Deputy under Secretary of
AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009                                                       23


     Defense (Environmental Security) memo, Guidance for Claiming Exemptions from the
     AFV Requirements of the EPAct of 23 Oct 97.
        1.13.1.1.1. A law enforcement vehicle may be:
           1.13.1.1.1.1. Any vehicle which is (1) specifically approved in an agency’s
           appropriation act for use in apprehension, pursuit, patrol or protection, or is
           routinely used for other law enforcement activities such as surveillance, or (2)
           covered by the current version of Item 17 or Item 17A, Federal Standard Number
           122, published by GSA.
           1.13.1.1.1.2. A leased vehicle equipped with at least the following components
           (1) for passenger automobiles, heavy-duty electrical, cooling and suspension
           systems, and at least the next more powerful engine than is standard for the
           automobile concerned, or (2) for light trucks, emergency warning lights or
           identification or markings such as ―police‖.
           1.13.1.1.1.3. An unmarked vehicle which is certified by the Secretary of the Air
           Force (SECAF) as essential for the safe and efficient performance of intelligence,
           counterintelligence, protective or other law enforcement duties.
        1.13.1.1.2. An emergency vehicle is any vehicle that is legally authorized by a
        government authority to exceed the speed limit to transport people and/or equipment
        to and from a situation in which speed is required to save lives or property, such as a
        rescue vehicle, fire truck or ambulance.
        1.13.1.1.3. Vehicles used for military purposes (exempt for national security
        reasons):
           1.13.1.1.3.1. Vehicles defined as ―tactical vehicles‖ are automatically exempt
           from EPAct AFV requirements and a national security exemption need not be
           claimed for them. LDVs similar to those driven by the general public are also
           automatically exempt as tactical vehicles if the vehicles have been modified (such
           as the addition of a 24-volt electrical system) to meet military specifications,
           unless the vehicle is normally used as an administrative vehicle.
           1.13.1.1.3.2. Administrative vehicles that may have to be deployed as part of a
           military operation will not automatically receive a national security exemption.
           Many vehicles can operate on alternative fuel when performing their
           administrative missions at home station and operate on a petroleum-based fuel
           when deployed as part of a military operation. A national security exemption
           should be claimed for such a vehicle only when there is substantial possibility that
           the vehicle will be deployed, and the vehicle could not be deployed if it was an
           AFV or the AFV attributes of the vehicle would negatively affect performance of
           its mission when deployed.
        1.13.1.1.4. MAJCOM Vehicle Management Staffs are encouraged to acquire
        alternative fueled law enforcement and emergency vehicles if such vehicles are
        capable of meeting mission requirements. Such AFVs will be counted as credits
        toward the total AFVs required under EPAct.
24                                           AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


        1.13.1.2. MAJCOM and installation Vehicle Management Staffs are responsible for
        requesting and prioritizing through the vehicle buy program the appropriate number of
        new Original Equipment Manufacturer (OEM) AFV purchases to meet annual EPAct and
        EO 13423 requirements. Any shortages in OEM AFV acquisitions should be
        compensated through additional AFV long-term leases.
            1.13.1.2.1. When purchasing new vehicles, all units must first consider AFVs or
            LSVs (see paragraph 1.26 regarding purchase of LSVs). When considering AFVs,
            the decision should be based on availability of alternative fuel infrastructure, either
            on-base or in the local area. After AFVs or LSVs, a Hybrid Electric Vehicle (HEV)
            should be the next consideration. The final choice should be a conventional vehicle
            (diesel is preferable if biodiesel (B20) is available locally). When purchasing diesel
            vehicles, B20 capable vehicles must be purchased when available. When an AFV,
            LSV or HEV is available to fill a vehicle authorization and a conventional vehicle is
            chosen instead, documented justification for choosing a conventional vehicle will be
            retained for the life of the vehicle.
            1.13.1.2.2. When leasing vehicles, MAJCOM and installation Vehicle Management
            Staffs are also responsible for ensuring that long-term lease requests (paragraph
            4.31) meet EPAct and EO 13423 requirements for each requesting base. In most
            cases, AFV leases will require funding from installation Operations and Maintenance
            (O&M) sources. (For information on GSA AFV leases contact GSA Fleet Services at
            gsafleet@gsa.gov).
        1.13.1.3. Once procured, AFVs count as credits toward EPAct requirements regardless
        of whether they are assigned to an MSA. Nevertheless, MAJCOM Vehicle Management
        Staffs are encouraged to assign AFVs where infrastructure is available or the AFVs can
        be used to leverage potential infrastructure.
        1.13.1.4. MAJCOM Vehicle Management Staffs must consider the vehicle’s engine
        configuration, ensuring they acquire the vehicle with the best fuel economy that meets the
        organization's mission requirements.
     1.13.2. Description of different types of AFVs and Electric Vehicles (EV). The Air Force's
     AFVs, whether purchased or leased, are bi-fuel, flex-fuel or a dedicated alternative fueled
     vehicle, i.e. CNG, propane, E85, etc. Electric Vehicles can either be dedicated or hybrid
     technology.
        1.13.2.1. Bi-fuel AFVs have two mutually exclusive fuel systems and are designed to
        operate on either gasoline or an alternative fuel such as Compressed Natural Gas (CNG)
        or Liquefied Petroleum Gas (LPG), also known as propane, using one fuel at a time.
        1.13.2.2. Flex-fuel AFVs are capable of using gasoline, and an alternative fuel such as
        E85 (85 percent ethanol and 15 percent gasoline), or a mixture of gasoline and alternative
        fuel through the same tank/system.
        1.13.2.3. Dedicated AFVs are vehicles that operate solely on an alternative fuel.
        1.13.2.4. EVs operate solely from power stored in a system of batteries.
        1.13.2.5. Hybrid-electric vehicles (HEVs) combine the benefits of gasoline or diesel
        engines and electric motors. HEVs can be configured to obtain different objectives, such
AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009                                                            25


     as improved fuel economy, increased power or additional auxiliary power for electronic
     devices and power tools.
  1.13.3. Infrastructure. Funding for development of on-base alternative fuel infrastructure is
  limited. The EPAct of 2005 mandates that agencies arrange for fueling of AFVs at
  commercial, federally and/ or publicly accessible facilities to the maximum extent practical.
  If such a station exists or is planned, AFVs are required to use a station if it is located within
  5 miles or 15 minutes from the installation IAW DOD 4500.36R. Locating alternative fuels
  for any location can be done using the DOE Alternative Fuel Site Locator:
  http://www.eere.energy.gov/afdc/index.html.
     1.13.3.1. If a commercial station proves impractical in terms of fuel costs, vehicle wear
     and manpower, a very advantageous means of acquiring fueling capability is through:
         1.13.3.1.1. Defense Logistics Agency (DLA) funded infrastructure.
             1.13.3.1.1.1. DLA’s Defense Energy Support Center (DESC) is responsible to
             fund for new fuels infrastructure holding fuel capitalized by the Defense Working
             Capital Fund IAW DODD 4140.25, DOD Management Policy for Energy
             Commodities and Related Services.
             1.13.3.1.1.2. Vehicle Management will identify fuel requirements to Fuels
             Management Flight (FMF) by grade, expected annual consumption and total
             vehicle fuel tank capacity.
             1.13.3.1.1.3. The FMF will analyze the base infrastructure and submit an AF
             Form 332, Base Civil Engineer Work Request, to Civil Engineering (CE)
             establishing the requirement for additional infrastructure based on the requirement
             for the total storage required by grade and type of fuel IAW Uniform Fire Code
             (UFC) 03-04-01.
             1.13.3.1.1.4. The CE will assess parking, traffic, tankage and dispensing
             equipment identified by the FMF IAW UFC 03-460-01, Design, Petroleum Fuel
             Facilities and CE guidance. The CE will prepare cost estimates and programming
             documentation for submission through the MAJCOM Fuel Office to Air Force
             Petroleum Office (AFPET) and the DESC IAW DESC programming guidance.
     1.13.3.2. Contractor-owned/maintained equipment agreement. Negotiating agreements
     with commercial fuel providers for alternative fuel stations can be complicated.
     Installations are encouraged to obtain access to commercial alternative fuel infrastructure
     through their own efforts if possible, but are advised to seek expert help from MAJCOM,
     WR-ALC and Air Force Civil Engineering Support Agency (AFCESA). Pitfalls that may
     be avoided through seeking expert help include disadvantageous agreements that require
     DOD to pay exorbitant fuel prices for long periods or agreements that provide for
     exorbitant penalties in the event that DOD installations fail to reach specified alternative
     fuel usage levels.
  1.13.4. Alternative Fuel Usage. EPAct of 2005 Section 701 dictates that all bi-/flex-fuel
  vehicles must use their designated alternative fuel 100 percent of the time or submit a waiver
  if unable to meet this requirement due to lack of alternative fuel infrastructure or
  unreasonably high cost of alternative fuel compared to gasoline. Once the Department of
26                                             AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


     Energy establishes the policy for submitting waivers, the VEMSO will work with the
     affected MAJCOMs to submit the appropriate paperwork. If alternative fuel infrastructure is
     in place and the cost of alternative fuel is not unreasonably high then the bi-/flex-fuel vehicle
     must operate on the alternative fuel system 100 percent of the time.
        1.13.4.1. If an AF-owned or leased AFV is within 5 miles or 15 minutes one way from a
        commercial off base alternative fuel station, the alternative fuel will be used 100% of the
        time. If on base alternative fuel E85 is available, AFVs will use ethanol 100% of the
        time.
        1.13.4.2. If an installation has a nearby public or private alternative fuel station but no
        formal agreement has been established for its use, the installation will establish an
        economically advantageous use agreement, as described in paragraph 1.13.3.2
        1.13.4.3. If an AFV is frequently driven off-base for substantial distances, it should be
        refueled at alternative fuel stations to the greatest extent practical. Such vehicles should
        always be equipped with a regional map or directory of appropriate alternative fuel
        stations.
        1.13.4.4. If the only available alternative fuel is on the commercial market, a DoD Fleet
        Credit Card will have to be used to refuel Air Force owned vehicles. If this scenario
        becomes necessary, establish procedures to ensure the fuel is uploaded into OLVIMS as
        an off-base fuels transaction. If the appropriate alternative fuel is available on base, the
        Vehicle Identification Link (VIL) key shall be coded so only the alternative fuel can be
        utilized IAW paragraph 4.5.1
        1.13.4.5. MAJCOM Fleet managers shall immediately implement a plan to clearly and
        conspicuously identify all flexible fuel vehicles to vehicle operators.
            1.13.4.5.1. This could be accomplished through the use of a sticker affixed near the
            fuel gauge or fuel tank filler neck combined with a yellow fuel cap or other noticeable
            methods of indicating that the vehicle should be fueled with the appropriate
            alternative fuel whenever practical. Note: Procedures for identifying E-85 GSA
            leased vehicles are currently being staffed by GSA for release. Until then, any
            identification of GSA leased E-85 vehicles should be accomplished in a manner that
            will not damage the vehicle in order to avoid potential bill back costs when the
            vehicle is returned to GSA.
            1.13.4.5.2. Vehicles that have not submitted for waiver under the EPAct 2005,
            Section 701 shall also have a map in the vehicle that indicates the location of all
            available alternative fueling infrastructure within five miles or 15 minutes of their
            normal operating location. There are several sources for such maps, including:
            http://www.afdc.energy.gov/afdc/stations/find_station.php.
            1.13.4.5.3. The fleet manager’s implementation plan shall also include training to all
            operators to ensure they understand the requirement to use the appropriate alternative
            fuel and shall clearly indicate that the price of the alternative fuel is not a factor in
            deciding to use it. Fleet managers will periodically check the data available to them to
            ensure this policy is being followed and conduct at least a semiannual assessment for
            sources of alternative fuels on the economy. For suggestions on how to track
            alternative fuel consumption, contact the VEMSO.
AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009                                                              27


1.14. Biodiesel (B20).
   1.14.1. B20 is not an alternative fuel but is classified by the DESC as a special fuel and aids
   DOD agencies to automatically reduce their dependency on foreign oil by 20 percent. B20 is
   a fuel blend consisting of 20-volume percent biodiesel and 80-volume percent diesel fuel
   (grades DL1/DL2 or LS1/LS2) that can be used in lieu of diesel fuel in compression ignition
   engines. The use of B20 not only provides an automatic 20 percent fossil fuel reduction, it
   also reduces air emissions and gives us credit towards the EPAct 75 percent AFV acquisition
   mandate. One energy credit can be obtained by the use of 2,250 gallons of B20 in diesel
   engine driven vehicles weighing more than 8,500 pounds.
   1.14.2. The use of B20 is essential in the Air Force's efforts to reduce its dependency on
   foreign oil. Regardless of the warranty implications, the Air Force mandates that B20 be
   used to the maximum extent possible. If a warranty condition develops due to the use of B20,
   then report the failure via the Air Force Vehicle Management Neighborhood so failures can
   be tracked and trends analyzed. Be sure to coordinate your failure reports with the local fuels
   management activity to ensure that no problems exist with the quality of B20 at the refueling
   station. Warranty repair costs that are not covered by the dealer will be absorbed by the local
   vehicle management activity.
   1.14.3. Additional information on B20 can be found in TO 42B1-1-1, Fuels for USAF Piston
   and Turbine Support Equipment and Administrative Vehicles and HQ AFPET Fuels
   Technical Letter 08-1, Handling of B20 Biodiesel Fuel Products.
1.15. Budget Guidance and Procedures. Host-tenant agreements identify responsibilities of
the host and tenant. The host base VFM or VMS assigns the priorities and services detailed in the
agreement. See AFI 25-201 for details on development of host-tenant agreements and AFI 65-
601v1, Budget Guidance and Procedures, for budget and reimbursement guidance. Each month
Vehicle Management and Analysis (VM&A) forwards the ? RAD6C (? = site code) disk to
Accounting and Finance or Defense Finance and Accounting Service (DFAS) to generate the
Motor Vehicle Reimburse/Refunds Billing List, PCN SH069-V12. This list shows all labor and
materials expended on each vehicle repaired. The VFM or VMS uses this report to verify
reimbursable and/or refundable expenses to be recouped. Normally military labor is not
reimbursable. Note: The VFM or VMS acts as a technical advisor when host-tenant agreements
are being made.
1.16. Environmental Program. AFPD 32-70, Environmental Quality, provides overall policy
guidance associated with the AF’s Environmental Quality Program. As stated in this policy
directive, ―The Air Force is committed to: Cleaning up environmental damage resulting from its
past activities; meeting all environmental standards applicable to its present operations; planning
its future activities to minimize environmental impacts; managing responsibly the irreplaceable
natural and cultural resources it holds in public trust; and eliminating pollution from its activities
wherever possible.‖ All AF functions are required to comply with the AF’s environmental
quality program and as such, Vehicle Management activities incur certain environmental
responsibilities, to include, but not limited to:
   1.16.1. The Clean Air Act (CAA) and Clean Air Act Amendments (CAAA) of 1990 deal
   with atmospheric pollution and are implemented at the federal, state and local level. The
   major areas of concern for Vehicle Management under this legislation are vehicle exhaust
28                                           AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


     emissions and ozone depleting chemicals such as Chlorofluorocarbons (CFC)-12 air
     conditioning refrigerant.
        1.16.1.1. The 1990 CAAA, states with non-attainment areas for transportation-related
        pollutants are required to implement inspection and maintenance programs (see Title 40
        Code of Federal Regulations (CFR) Part 51). The purpose of inspection and maintenance
        programs is to identify and assure the repair of, in-use vehicles that are emitting
        excessive pollutants. Federal fleet vehicles, except for military tactical vehicles, are
        subject to inspection and maintenance requirements. A military tactical vehicle is one
        designed or modified to military specifications to meet direct transportation support of
        combat, tactical or relief operations, or for training of personnel for such purposes.
            1.16.1.1.1. The VFM or VMS should cooperate with the state to test Federal fleet
            vehicles and submit applicable reports. Exclude military tactical vehicles and any
            other vehicles exempted under state law. These may include, for example, emergency
            vehicles, AFVs and certain newer model years.
        1.16.1.2. Title 40, Code of Regulation CFR, Part 82, Subpart B, establishes standards
        and requirements for servicing Motor Vehicle Air Conditioners (MVACs). This rule
        requires that only certified technicians, using certified equipment which has been
        registered with the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) service or maintain MVACs.
        Obtain technician certification through one of several EPA-approved mechanics-testing
        and/or certifying programs. Proof of certification must be available on site at all times.
        CFC-134A is the only authorized refrigerant when servicing vehicles unless you have in-
        house recovered CFC-12 which you can use. If contract repairs are needed for a CFC-12
        system they MUST be converted to CFC-134A. If MVAC repairs require purchasing
        CFC-12 it MUST be converted to CFC-134A.
        1.16.1.3. Vehicle emission control devices are maintained and inspected according to
        technical directives, manufacturer’s recommendations, and federal, state, local or host
        nation laws. All Vehicle Management activities use a program of regularly scheduled
        diagnostic tests to make sure that all engines comply with manufacturer’s specifications.
        This test is usually done as part of the PM&I or when a major tune-up is accomplished.
        Inspect emission control devices when a vehicle is in for PM&I and see that the
        necessary work is done to ensure proper operation.
     1.16.2. The Resource Conservation and Recovery Act (RCRA) and EO 13423, legislate and
     implement pollution prevention, recycling of solid and liquid waste and affirmative
     procurement of environmentally preferable products (manufactured from recycled materials)
     and services.
        1.16.2.1. EO 13423 and RCRA Section 6002 also require procurement of re-refined oil
        and retread or post-consumer content tires during acquisition and routine maintenance of
        vehicles, to include AFVs. DOD policy dictates that the DLA automatically substitute re-
        refined oil when activities order certain grades of commercial oil. DOD activities
        purchasing oil from sources other than DLA must comply with the legal requirements to
        use re-refined oil and activities are reminded that the central supply system is the
        preferred method of purchasing supplies. Do not purchase or arrange for purchase of, any
        virgin motor oil when a re-refined alternative is readily available and meets vehicle
        manufacturer recommended performance standards. NOTE: Model year 2007 and newer
AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009                                                        29


     diesel engines with new emissions requirements should use virgin API CJ-4 motor oil
     until it is available as a re-refined product.
     1.16.2.2. Approved re-refined oil, which can be used in most AF vehicles, will meet
     current American Petroleum Institute (API) standards, evidenced by the "Starburst"
     symbol on the container.
     1.16.2.3. The DLA will submit the aggregate AF volume and cost of re-refined oil for
     each reporting period. Units not purchasing oils through the DLA will account for
     quantity purchased and dollar amount expended for locally procured oils.
     1.16.2.4. Consistent with Executive Order 13101 and RCRA section 6962, in acquiring
     and maintaining motor vehicles, agencies shall acquire and use retread tires, when such
     products are reasonably available and meet applicable performance standards.
  1.16.3. The Pollution Prevention Act of 1990 established that pollution should be prevented
  or reduced at the source; that pollution which cannot be prevented should be recycled in an
  environmentally safe manner; that pollution which cannot be prevented or recycled should be
  treated in an environmentally safe manner; and that disposal or other release to the
  environment should be employed only as a last resort and conducted in an environmentally
  safe manner. Conduct vehicle management operations in keeping with this hierarchy of
  actions.
     1.16.3.1. IAW Section 6014 of the Safe, Accountable, Flexible, Efficient Transportation
     Equity Act (SAFETEA): A Legacy for Users (SAFETEA-LU) it is the responsibility of
     Federal Agencies to purchase recycled/reclaimed coolant where it meets their
     performance needs and is available. Units are required to recycle their antifreeze.
     Additionally, Vehicle Management will assist other units on base who do not have the
     capability to recycle antifreeze where possible. If antifreeze cannot be recycled, a waiver
     must be requested from AF/A4LE with justification, coordinated through the respective
     MAJCOM Vehicle Management Staff. Recycled/reclaimed antifreeze can only be
     purchased after existing supplies of recycled antifreeze are exhausted unless one of the
     three below exemptions applies.
         1.16.3.1.1. When the need for total antifreeze replacement is required or deemed
         necessary (IAW TO 36-1-191), units must purchase and/or utilize existing
         recycled/reclaimed antifreeze stocks. Note: Recycled/reclaimed antifreeze is not
         always compatible with existing virgin coolants and the coolant system must be
         completely drained and purged prior to the use of recycled/reclaimed antifreeze. Be
         sure to check manufacturer's specifications prior to adding or replacing
         recycled/reclaimed antifreeze. The only exceptions to the rule are:
            1.16.3.1.1.1. Using recycled/reclaimed antifreeze would void the warranty.
            However, recycled/reclaimed antifreeze may be used when it meets the
            manufacturer's specifications. Note:    After the warranty period expires
            recycled/reclaimed antifreeze MUST be used exclusively.
            1.16.3.1.1.2. Some heavy equipment manufacturers do not recommend the use of
            reclaimed engine coolants, particularly in engines meeting the new Tier II and
            Tier III emissions requirements, e.g., the Halverson loader coolant requirement
            prevents the use of recycled/reclaimed antifreeze. Be sure to check manufacturer's
30                                            AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


               specifications on the use of recycled/reclaimed antifreeze.
           1.16.3.1.2. The USAF will only use reclaimed engine coolant. The coolant must
           either be purchased as reclaimed or the unit must reclaim their own coolant. Virgin
           coolant is authorized for use if required by the manufacturer during the warranty
           period. If reclaimed coolant is unavailable the unit must have an approved waiver
           from VEMSO. DLA’s Defense Supply Center Richmond (DSCR) sells both
           reclaimed and virgin engine coolant. DSCR requires that reclaimed engine coolant
           contain a minimum of 50 percent reclaimed ethylene glycol. DSCR offers reclaimed
           engine coolants containing either 100 percent reclaimed ethylene glycol or 60 percent
           and 50 percent blends of reclaimed and virgin ethylene glycol. As shown in Table
           1.1, Reclaimed Engine Coolant Available from DLA, each blend is available in 1-
           gallon, 5-gallon and 55-gallon drum packaging, for a total of nine stock items. These
           products can be used in administrative vehicles, construction and material handling
           vehicles and equipment, and military ground combat tactical vehicles and equipment.
           DSCR currently contracts with Leeder Automotive for reclaimed engine coolant.

           1.16.3.1.3. Units must track all recycled/reclaimed antifreeze consumed and cost for
           the entire FY and report data IAW paragraph 4.75.5
        1.16.3.2. Oil analysis is a method to monitor actual contamination of oil. It helps to
        prevent unnecessary oil changes by providing the user the capability to determine when
        oil changes are needed based on the oil’s actual condition, not on the length of time the
        oil has been used. MAJCOM Vehicle Management Staffs have the authority to
        implement oil analysis programs for all or specific segments of their assigned vehicle
        fleets at their discretion. Oil analysis is considered a waste stream reduction tool. It may
        increase the Vehicle Management activity’s workload.
        1.16.3.3. Use base Hazardous Material Pharmacy (HAZMART), in accordance with AFI
        32-7086, Hazardous Material Management, and in consultation with the base HAZMAT
AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009                                                        31


      Management Process Team, to the maximum extent practical as a means to reduce waste
      of bulk Hazardous Materials (HAZMAT) when their shelf life expires. Within the
      framework of the pharmacy program, ensure vehicle management processes are
      authorized by the Hazardous Material Management Process (HMMP) Team in
      accordance with AFI 32-7086, to enable establishment of a set amount of HAZMAT
      needed to perform their operation and receive measured amounts of this material on an
      as-required basis; as well as to ensure Environmental Safety and Occupational Health
      (ESOH) controls are established. Note: Vehicle specific HAZMAT not managed by the
      HAZMART will be controlled IAW pharmacy practices.
      1.16.3.4. Follow local directives and laws when hazardous waste disposal is required.
      The VFM or VMS establish a CE-coordinated OI on pollution prevention and on
      hazardous waste management.
   1.16.4. Only lead-free wheel weights are authorized for purchase. Lead wheel weights will
   no longer be purchased and will be replaced with lead-free wheel weights through attrition.
   See Table 1.2 Lead-Free Wheel Weights Available Through the Supply System.
1.17. Prohibited Vehicle Management Activities. What the Vehicle Management Flight Must
Not Do:
   1.17.1. Do not service, repair or garage the following vehicles in an AF appropriated fund
   facility:
      1.17.1.1. Vehicles disposed of to the Defense Reutilization and Marketing Service
      (DRMS). Exception: CAP vehicles as outlined in AFPD 10-27, Civil Air Patrol.
      1.17.1.2. Privately Owned Vehicles (POVs) or non-appropriated fund motor vehicles.
      Exception: National Forest Service vehicles.
      1.17.1.3. Leased vehicles. This applies to both GSA and commercial leased vehicles.
      Exception: Those Vehicle Management activities authorized by HQ USAF/A4LE (see
      paragraph 4.34.3).
   1.17.2. Do not use government owned tools, equipment or supplies purchased from
   appropriated funds to service or repair non-appropriated fund or private vehicles. Exception:
   Those authorized by HQ USAF/A4LE.
   1.17.3. Do not park POVs in the Vehicle Management compound. Exception: Written
   approval of the Logistics Readiness Squadron (LRS) Commander or equivalent.
1.18. Productivity Measurement. The Office of the Secretary of Defense has issued guidance
(DOD 4500.36-R) to measure and monitor labor hour productivity within Vehicle Management
and Vehicle Operations activities for the following purposes:
   1.18.1. To support vehicle repair decisions and develop budgets at base-level.
   1.18.2. To determine the number of delayed work-hours attributed to lack of personnel
   available to work on vehicles when needed.
   1.18.3. To identify training needs, job performance evaluations, shop equipment, tools and
   facility upgrades.
32                                            AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


     1.18.4. To determine the management efficiency of individuals, work centers, management
     activities and MAJCOMs.
        1.18.4.1. (Added-PACAF) . All Japan and Korea bases will delete all civilian labor
        transactions before submitting the On-Line Vehicle Interactive Management System
        (OLVIMS) accounting and finance data file for reimbursement. Only parts transactions
        will be reimbursed for bases in Japan and Korea.
     1.18.5. To determine if AF Vehicle Management personnel are being used efficiently.
1.19. Serviceability Standards. Minimum serviceability standards for day-to-day operations
are determined by the VFM or VMS based on his/her experience and applicable technical
reference. Ensure AF vehicles are maintained in safe and serviceable condition.
     1.19.1. The AF accepts the idea that as vehicles age a certain amount of wear and tear will
     occur and that AF vehicles will not be maintained in a like-new condition. Example: The
     condition of a vehicle in its 6th year of use is not comparable with the condition of a new
     vehicle. To be serviceable, a vehicle must first be safe and function as designed. Deficiencies
     affecting safety are not acceptable regardless of whether the vehicle is old or new.
     1.19.2. Judge serviceability using the following factors:
        1.19.2.1. Age and mileage of the vehicle.
        1.19.2.2. Requirement for the type of vehicle and job it is designed to do.
        1.19.2.3. Remaining service life.
        1.19.2.4. Value of use returned in comparison with the cost of needed repairs.
1.20. Vehicle Abuse and Accidents. Vehicle abuse is normally considered damage caused by
willful or negligent acts of improper operation or care. Vehicle accident is basically a result of a
collision. The LRS Commander or equivalent establishes local reporting procedures and these
procedures must be fully supported by all commanders to be totally effective. Using
organizations fund for costs associated with government owned, lease and rental, vehicle abuse
and/or accident damage. Note: Publish these implementing instructions at wing–level or
equivalents.
     1.20.1. All Vehicle Management O&M funds expended for vehicle accident and abuse repair
     costs, including contract cost, will be reimbursed to Vehicle Management by the owning
     organization, or the organization responsible for the damage if not the owner.
     1.20.2. Where responsibility cannot be determined, the owning organization will be
     responsible for reimbursement. The using organization investigates all damage not
     attributable to fair wear and tear IAW AFMAN 23-220, Reports of Survey for Air Force
     Property. Vehicle Management notifies the applicable commander or equivalent, and Vehicle
     Control Officer (VCO) or Vehicle Control Noncommissioned Control Officer (VCNCO) of
     the unit involved, along with Base Legal, Finance and Safety Office (usually by form letter)
     as applicable.
     1.20.3. In the event of a government owned vehicle accident, include a copy of the accident
     report for the Base Safety Office’s use if available. Vehicle Management commences repair
     within a locally established time frame unless notified otherwise by the using organization or
     Report of Survey (ROS) investigating official. Note: If a government owned vehicle caused
AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009                                                           33


   a fatality or was involved in a fatality or an accident with private property the Base Legal
   Office must release the vehicle before repairs commence.
   1.20.4. Incidents are classified as damage caused by acts of nature, natural disasters,
   mechanical failures or other phenomenon that in no way could have been avoided by safe
   operation or adequate vehicle care during non-use. Cases of incidents may also include
   damage resulting from normal use for the vehicle’s intended purpose (e.g., snow removal or
   heavy construction work). Using organizations are not required to reimburse for damage
   caused by incidents.
1.21. Vehicle Fleet Categorization. The AF owned vehicle fleet consists of a multitude of
makes, models, manufacturers and types. In order to better articulate the relationship of different
vehicle types to the AF missions, the categories below are provided to capture this relationship.
   1.21.1. Sortie Generation. Vehicles in this category are flight line assets associated with
   direct mission support. Representative examples include: Aircraft refuelers, aircraft cargo
   loaders, tow tractors, etc.
   1.21.2. Sortie Sustainment. This vehicle set includes vehicles required to operate an air base
   or installation, such as air crew and troop carrying vehicles, crash rescue and fire fighting
   trucks, emergency response vehicles, sweepers, etc.
   1.21.3. Tactical Vehicles. Tactical vehicles include all vehicles procured as military design
   (M-series) assets.
   1.21.4. Material Handling Equipment (MHE). This category encompasses all 463L and
   MHE vehicles and vehicular equipment: Forklifts, rollerized trailers, container handlers, etc.
   1.21.5. Base Maintenance/Construction Equipment. Included are vehicles and vehicular
   equipment used primarily by CE in the construction, maintenance and upkeep of an air base
   or installation.
1.22. Vehicle Fleet Growth Policy The AF vehicle fleet represents a tremendous infrastructure
investment for the corporate AF. The vehicle buy program provides funding for programmatic
replacement of assets, but is not funded to purchase new requirements for vehicles. MAJCOM,
FOA and DRU logistical support staffs must closely scrutinize any request to increase the size of
their respective vehicle fleets. Requests to increase vehicle fleet size must be supported by a
verifiable mission change, e.g., Operational Plan (OPLAN), Provisional Plan (PPLAN), etc. The
functional community requesting additional vehicle authorizations will fund the initial purchase
of the vehicles, and advocate additional funding in the out-years of the Program Objective
Memorandum (POM) for increased vehicle buy replacement dollars. Any non-mission change
increase will be offset by adjustments to other vehicle authorizations to preclude an overall
increase in authorizations. All adjustments to authorization quantities must be approved by
AFELM VEMSO or other AF/A4LE designated enterprise management authority.
   1.22.1. Non-cataloged vehicle request. Prior to requesting cataloging action, MAJCOM,
   FOA and DRU logistical support staffs will consider offsets, current authorizations and right
   sizing of the vehicle requirement. Staffs will also coordinate with the appropriate Item
   Manager/Equipment Specialist to determine if there is an AF stock listed vehicle to satisfy
   the requirement. If a requirement for cataloging action still exists after completing the
   considerations and coordination above, staffs will submit an AF Form 601 with item
34                                          AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


     specifications and justification to VEMSO. VEMSO will review and submit approved
     requests to the AF Allowance Standard (AS) Manager for cataloging action and AS
     inclusion.
     1.22.2. (Added-PACAF) . Request to establish new vehicle authorizations without a
     verifiable/programmable HQ Air Force/MAJCOM directed mission change; e.g., Operation
     Plan (OPLAN), Programming Plan (PPLAN), etc. must include an offsetting decrease from
     other wing/command authorizations of equal procurement value. All authorization increase
     requests submitted due to a mission change must have the mission change documentation
     attached.
1.23. Vehicle Validation Visit (VVV). The AF vehicle fleet represents a significant investment
for the corporate AF. Validated vehicle authorizations define the overall AF requirement for
vehicles and must be periodically revalidated to ensure that installation vehicle needs are
economically being met, at the least possible cost. VEMSO is responsible for performing a VVV
of each fleet every 3 to 5 years depending on established trigger points. Responsibility for VVVs
previously resided with the MAJCOM and it is anticipated there will be a 2 year transfer plan
implemented to migrate this requirement and its associated cost to VEMSO.
1.24. VEMSO. VEMSO acts as an adjunct staff to all the MAJCOM Vehicle Management
Staffs. VEMSO is responsible for all data collection and draft report generation required above
the MAJCOM-level. While they are responsible for the data collection and draft report
generation, each MAJCOM finalizes their own written narrative to those reports as required.
Responsibilities and procedures for VEMSO are provided in Chapter 8.
1.25. Wartime Readiness. It is AF policy to have motor vehicles, manpower, tools, facilities,
and shop equipment on hand to support the USAF War and Mobilization Plan (WMP).
Contingency operations are addressed in Chapter 2.
1.26. LSV. Each Air Logistics Center (ALC), MAJCOM, Direct Reporting Unit (DRU) and
base LRS will have the same ―cradle to grave‖ management of LSV as they do with the
conventional fleet. MAJCOMs, DRUs and the Air Force District of Washington (AFDW)
residing on military installations will replace existing general-purpose assets to the greatest
extent possible with LSVs. The goal is to replace 30 percent or more of all applicable LDVs
with more fuel efficient LSVs by FY 2012. However, all echelons are highly encouraged to
seize additional opportunities wherever possible. This will be a Unit Compliance Inspection
(UCI) checklist item to ensure enforcement of this new procurement policy. See Table 1.4 for
the new categories and applicable NSNs.

   1.26.1. For those LSVs purchased with unit funds prior to June 2006, organizations may
   continue to manage these assets as equipment, in which case they will be considered non-
   Registered Equipment Management System (REMS) reportable LSVs. However, if the
   owning organizations currently have vacant authorizations, the Base Fleet Manager will
   consider filling the authorizations with the available non-REMS reportable LSVs. The Base
   Fleet Manager and the owning organization’s VCO or VCNCO will coordinate these actions.
   1.26.2. The WR-ALC (580 CBSS) reclassified LSVs from ―equipment‖ to ―registered
   vehicles‖ to include creation of new NSNs for the seven sub-categories listed in Table 1.4
   LSVs are identified as budget code ―V‖ and classified as centrally procured vehicles, thus
   removing these assets from ASC 036 to 019. The current AF vehicle acquisition process
   now includes the procurement of these assets. Wings and bases are no longer authorized to
   purchase LSVs without first coordinating through the respective base-level and then
   MAJCOM Vehicle Management Staffs
      1.26.2.1. All conventional vehicle authorizations will be scrutinized to determine
      potential candidates for conversion to LSVs. Unit mission requirements and past
      utilization will be the key indicators along with the VFM/VMS recommendations
      when vehicle replacement decisions are made.
      1.26.2.2. Base/unit level Fleet Managers should use the following preferred approach
      when determining which general purpose vehicle authorizations can be converted to LSV
      authorizations. The goal is to convert 30 or more percent of the selected authorizations to
      LSVs.
          1.26.2.2.1. Begin by identifying general purpose vehicles, by registration number
          that meet the following criteria:
               1.26.2.2.1.1. Vehicle management codes B168, B170, B176, B178, B180, B200,
               B204, B211 or B227
               1.26.2.2.1.2. - Driven 7.5K miles or less over the past 12 months
               1.26.2.2.1.3. Load capacity of 1.5T or less
               1.26.2.2.1.4. Assigned organization, user and support area is on-base
               1.26.2.2.1.5. Operates on roadways with 35MPH maximum speed limit and may
               occasionally cross roadways with higher speeds
               1.26.2.2.1.6. Vacant authorizations not previously identified for vehicle
               procurement in your annual vehicle buy submission NOTE: Exceptions should
               be made for first responder and primary C2 vehicle authorizations
36                                          AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009


            1.26.2.2.2. Once target list of authorizations has been developed, group them by
            organizations and schedule interviews with unit VCO/VCNCOs and flight Chief. It is
            imperative Fleet Managers discuss changes with the units that will receive the LSVs.
            1.26.2.2.3. When conducting interviews, be sure to share the following with the unit
            representatives:
               1.26.2.2.3.1. In most cases, LSVs cost nearly 20 to 50% less to purchase than GP
               counterparts.
               1.26.2.2.3.2. LSVs get almost double the fuel economy of GP vehicles.
               1.26.2.2.3.3. LSVs are very economical to maintain and are reliable assets.
               1.26.2.2.3.4. LSVs are governed at 25MPH by federal law.
               1.26.2.2.3.5. LSVs meet Federal Motor Vehicle Safety standards.
               1.26.2.2.3.6. LSVs are bare bones mission support assets with limited comfort
               elements such as extensive leg room or power steering.
            1.26.2.2.4. Once interviews are completed and a minimum of 30% of the
            authorizations from the management code list above have been identified for
            conversion, forward the results and authorization change request to your respective
            MAJCOM.
            1.26.2.2.5. MAJCOMs should publish instructions to ensure the changes are reflected
            in the FY08 vehicle buy program submission.
        1.26.2.3. WR-ALC centrally procures all LSVs and LSV management and maintenance
        is the responsibility of the local Vehicle Management Flight. All aspects of maintenance
        on non-REMS reportable LSVs will remain the responsibility of owning organizations.
1.27. Sport Utility Vehicle (SUV) Authorizations. The following paragraphs establish Air
Force policy regarding SUV Authorizations.
     1.27.1. DOD 4500.36-R and this AFI requires we authorize only the minimum number and
     minimum size vehicles to meet mission needs.
     1.27.2. Conversion of any type of vehicle authorization to an SUV will not be approved to
     support AFV initiatives or to meet mandated fossil fuel acquisition requirements.
     1.27.3. Average purchase cost for an SUV is $7K higher than a sedan/station wagon and
     GSA lease cost is $1.6K more annually.
     1.27.4. SUVs are ―gas guzzlers‖ and each authorization awarded detracts from compliance
     with EO 13423 and EPAct 2005 guidance to reduce fossil fuel consumption.
     1.27.5. Sedans have historically provided adequate mission support for key operations and
     senior leaders. Example, MSG/CC On-Scene Commander (OSC) duties/responsibilities do
     not justify an SUV authorization.
     1.27.6. An occasional need for 4X4 capability does not support an upgrade to a fulltime
     SUV authorization (OSC duties considered here).
AFI23-302_PACAFSUP_I 23 NOVEMBER 2009                                                       37


   1.27.7. Public perception: a vehicle authorization upgrade or conversion to SUV for senior
   leaders and group commanders can easily be viewed as a perk associated with rank or
   position.
   1.27.8. If a verifiable mission requirement warrants conversion to an SUV authorization then
   the SUV size will be limited to the minimum body size and maximum fuel efficiency.
   MAJCOM Fleet Managers must adhere to the maximum Gross Vehicle Weight (GVW)
   restrictions listed in Table 1.3, Sedan to SUV Conversion. Note: MAJCOMs will identify
   SUV authorization, user assignment and utilization as UCI checklist item(s).
   1.27.9. For positions in the ranks of Lieutenant General and below, SUVs, minivans or
   crossovers will not exceed 5,400 lbs GVW. In addition, sedans will be limited to compact
   size as per Federal Regulations. Exceptions to this policy are limited. Medium sized SUVs
   and crossovers (GVW of 5,401 – 6,799 lbs) require SECAF approval before acquisition or
   lease is allowed. Large SUVs and crossovers (GVW of 6,800 lbs or higher) are strictly
   reserved for SECDEF, Service Secretaries and Four Star Officers. Note: Per SECAF
   memorandum dated 27 Mar 2009, the AF/A4/7 has been delegated the authority for Class III
   exceptions to policy.
